<div id="review_1720198278" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1720198278" />
    <a title="Ron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43881851-ron"><img alt="Ron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557265090p2/43881851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1720198278?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron" href="/user/show/43881851-ron">Ron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780399133145" href="/book/show/155213.The_Tommyknockers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1720198278" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1103263547702808786"><i>
  <blockquote>Late last night and the night before, Tommyknockers, Tommyknockers, knocking at the door. I want to go out, don't know if I can, 'cause I'm so afraid of the Tommyknocker man</blockquote>
</i><br/>So that’s a little creepy. Ever heard that childhood poem? I had not. Nor have I read much of H.P. Lovecraft, the horror author who influenced King’s writing of The Tommyknockers. King has said that his idea for the novel was based from “The Colour Out of Space”, a short story by Lovecraft. Tommyknockers is not the only n</span>
  <span id="freeText1103263547702808786" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>Late last night and the night before, Tommyknockers, Tommyknockers, knocking at the door. I want to go out, don't know if I can, 'cause I'm so afraid of the Tommyknocker man</blockquote>
</i><br />So that’s a little creepy. Ever heard that childhood poem? I had not. Nor have I read much of H.P. Lovecraft, the horror author who influenced King’s writing of The Tommyknockers. King has said that his idea for the novel was based from “The Colour Out of Space”, a short story by Lovecraft. Tommyknockers is not the only novel written by King, or many an author for that matter, that owe a debt to the man from the past.<br /><br />Strange things happen (a very Stephen King statement if there ever was one) after Bobbi Thompson starts digging up her backyard. After tripping over, then touching a piece of metal, buried in the ground back there, she becomes consumed with unearthing the thing that seems to have no end. Whatever it is, it’s big, and really King doesn’t try to hide what it might be. He pretty much tells the reader right off. What he does hide is just why and how it has taken control of people’s lives. Oh yeah, and most importantly, what’s the end game. This is interesting, sometimes fun and a little wacky. Once again, things happen that personally I could not have thought up (that’s why I read folks). People do some of the craziest things here. At this point, I was reminded of books like The Invasion of the Body Snatchers and The Puppet Masters. But King’s take on the theme is unique, never a copy.<br /><br />Like many of his books, subtly or not, King touches on the certain things affecting our world, whether it is war, or guns, or whatever. Here in The Tommyknockers, it is Nuclear Power. Is it good? Is it bad? The Chernobyl crisis had just occurred in Russia. Add to that the partial nuclear meltdown at Three Mile Island in 1979. It was on his mind, and shaped the story. Is that why the color green is used here? I don’t know, but green is everywhere.<br /><br /><u>The lowdown:</u> Quite a number of fans have said that The Tommyknockers is among their least favorite King novels. Me, I liked it. It not his best and King uses up a lot of pages before the story gets to really rockin’. I found the final 150 pages to be an absolute thrill ride. From the point that Gard walks into the Tommyknocker shed to the last page was simply outstanding, and I experienced so many feelings for this character nicknamed Gard.<br /><br />One of last thing. King has not-so-subtly hidden a load of “easter eggs” throughout the story. I can’t recall another book of King’s that has cross-referenced so many of his other works, and personal likes. Oh, and a buddy-read increases the fun of the egg hunt, as well as the terror of the dig. <b>QED</b> folks.<br /><br /><i>Shout out and a thanks to my buddies in the Eclectic Club!</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1103263547702808786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2510097116" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2510097116" />
    <a title="James Hartley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24237425-james-hartley"><img alt="James Hartley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1489914508p2/24237425.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2510097116?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Hartley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Hartley" href="/user/show/24237425-james-hartley">James Hartley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2510097116" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3460597646166066129">This is a fascinating book on many levels. <br/>Written at the height of King´s various addictions (with cotton wool stuffed up his nose to stop him bleeding onto the typewriter, apparently) - he wouldn´t publish again for another two years - it´s a look at what happens to a writer when they go out of control. It´s almost a master-class, writing-wise, on what NOT to do, but it´s loveable and fascinating: a drunk wheeling around a bar telling great stories in a scary, about-to-fall-over way. A coke fi</span>
  <span id="freeText3460597646166066129" style="display:none">This is a fascinating book on many levels. <br />Written at the height of King´s various addictions (with cotton wool stuffed up his nose to stop him bleeding onto the typewriter, apparently) - he wouldn´t publish again for another two years - it´s a look at what happens to a writer when they go out of control. It´s almost a master-class, writing-wise, on what NOT to do, but it´s loveable and fascinating: a drunk wheeling around a bar telling great stories in a scary, about-to-fall-over way. A coke fiend´s eyes as they come up from yet another line at four in the morning, finger-wagging, wanting to add some detail to some random aside they were telling you a moment ago which also incorporates a rule for understanding humanity.<br />King was obviously a tree-killing, money-making machine at this point which is why no editor or well-read English grad posing in the office dared to step in and cut his ranting, rambling, repetitive, crazed manuscript down to size. That the publishers were happy to put this out in the state it was published in and coin it in just the same tells you everything you need to know about the industry: it´s about the money, baby!<br />King has since called this "an awful book" and, like Cujo, probably can´t remember much about writing it. Perhaps it is bad - it certainly is in places - but King is not a bad writer which means there are books within the book, sections, which shimmer like jewels floating in a green-smoking cesspit. <br />I loved Gard, the boozy writer (written full-on, no holds barred, as though King were putting himself and his problems in the middle of the book and daring them to win), especially his pissed-up campus confrontation with a nuclear-power salesman. There are parts of the book, aspects of it, that shine like the slopes of the submerged ship: sleek, otherwordly, delightful, shocking and entertaining. <br />Although, notoriously, there are pages and pages of meandering, unnecessary dialogue and description, what tends to grate most over the length of the book is King´s seeming insistence on building up to a climax or confrontation or revelation which never comes. I liked the ending, though, though plenty didn´t, but constantly being brought to a point where something interesting was about to happen but didn´t was the type of literary teasing which leads one to think, "You know what? There are plenty of other books that´ll give me thrills so go screw yourself!"<br />The book didn´t scare or me, or even revolt me, so I couldn´t recommend it as either horror or (definitely not) thriller. The Tommyknockers rhyme itself is always, when it pops up, cringey and mawkish. What I would say, though, that the novel is a fascinating document on many other levels - as near a view into the world of King´s true, unedited imagination of that time as we´ll ever get, for example. <br />I was left open-mouthed by some of the pages - not for descriptions of vomit or aliens but just that they existed at all. That an agent hadn´t said anything. That a editor hadn´t said anything. That the publishers hadn´t said anything. That the reviewers gave it "King´s Best!" bullshit. That the readers lapped it up. Truly a world gone mad. Good on King´s wife for getting hold of him after it, at least, and getting her husband back.<br />Having said all that, for me The Tommyknockers will always have a special place in my heart because it fits into a weird (not precious, I promise) habit I have of liking what many people regard as the worst works of art in people´s canons, especially in music. I think Let´s Dance is Bowie´s best album, for example. I think Human Touch is Springsteen´s best album, the Bee Gees are better than the Beach Boys and, in movies, that GI Jane is Ridley Scott´s best film. I think it´s because I like the humanness of all of those ´works´; that element of crappness. I distrust perfection. I like to see failure and wrong-ness in works of art because that´s what I see in life. <br />Whatever it is, I recommend you read this book. <br />It´s perfectly imperfect.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3460597646166066129" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1665857516" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1665857516" />
    <a title="Bill Khaemba" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41380936-bill-khaemba"><img alt="Bill Khaemba" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1561102062p2/41380936.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1665857516?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Khaemba" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Khaemba" href="/user/show/41380936-bill-khaemba">Bill Khaemba</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/12412140-the-tommyknockers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41380936-bill-khaemba?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41380936-bill-khaemba?shelf=horror-scary">horror-scary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41380936-bill-khaemba?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1665857516" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11087185617658809130"><strong>
  <em>Late last night and the night before,</em>
</strong><br/><br/><strong>
  <em>Tommyknockers, Tommyknockers, knocking at the door.</em>
</strong><br/><br/><strong>
  <em>I want to go out, don't know if I can,</em>
</strong><br/><strong>
  <em>'Cause I'm so afraid of the Tommyknocker man.</em>
</strong><br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1474049610ra/20547940.gif' alt='Image result for checks under the bed gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><del>By The way, that jingle is really creepy if it was sung by a kid</del><br/>Something is creeping up in Haven, the atmosphere has rapidly changed after 
  <strong>Bobbi Anderson</strong>
 stumbles upon something in the woods… It will alter the normalcy of this small town in a very weird way.<br/><br/><em>Before anything this book was another successful b</em></span>
  <span id="freeText11087185617658809130" style="display:none"><strong>
  <em>Late last night and the night before,</em>
</strong><br><br><strong>
  <em>Tommyknockers, Tommyknockers, knocking at the door.</em>
</strong><br><br><strong>
  <em>I want to go out, don't know if I can,</em>
</strong><br><strong>
  <em>'Cause I'm so afraid of the Tommyknocker man.</em>
</strong><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1474049610ra/20547940.gif" alt="Image result for checks under the bed gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><del>By The way, that jingle is really creepy if it was sung by a kid</del><br>Something is creeping up in Haven, the atmosphere has rapidly changed after 
  <strong>Bobbi Anderson</strong>
 stumbles upon something in the woods… It will alter the normalcy of this small town in a very weird way.<br><br><em>Before anything this book was another successful buddy read, shout out to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/20625013-liz" rel="nofollow noopener">Liz</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/43881851-ron" rel="nofollow noopener">Ron</a> &amp; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/33269229-paul" rel="nofollow noopener">Paul</a>  :) Cheers to another trip down the pages</em>.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1469822019ra/19862144.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Firstly, this book gets a really bad rep for some apparent reason but in my opinion, it is unjustified. The average rating kind of lowered my expectation for this book and it was quite enjoyable. It was classic <strong>King</strong> and his take on the <strong>Sci-Fi</strong> genre, yes I am not an expert on the genre but I am the die-hard fan of King’s writing. The book had a slow burn creepy feeling as you flip through the pages… I was always anticipating something to pounce on the characters, it had a successful build up that paid in the end. The book proved to me such an awesome <em>buddy-read</em> choice because of the small cameo of different characters, quotes or little bit of hints from other King books<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1474049610ra/20547941.gif" alt="Image result for between the lines\ gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><blockquote>
  <strong>
    <em>
      “And I believe happiness is the exact opposite of sadness, bitterness, and hatred: happiness should remain unexamined as long as possible.”
    </em>
  </strong>
</blockquote><br>I will have to admit that unlike my reading buddies <strong>my experience with the book was not so smooth</strong>, Some parts of the book seemed dragged out and could have been cut short. The introduction of many characters was overwhelming at times but it wasn’t that bad as the “<del>critics</del>” point out.<br><blockquote>
  <strong>
    <em>
      “So what he supposed to do? Grab Bobbie's axe and make like Jack Nicholson in The Shinning? He could see it. Smash, crash, bash: Heeeeeeere's GARDENER!”
    </em>
  </strong>
</blockquote><br>The characters were complex and alive as with every King book, he wrote this book at a very dark time in his life and some characters mirrored his real life struggles, especially with alcoholism. It was also refreshing to see a main female character but what drove the book was the need to find out…<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1471503382i/20062528.gif" alt="Image result for What the hell is going on gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><em>I would recommend this to those who enjoyed Stranger Things or have the craving for that 80s aesthetic with that creepy King original style this was solid.</em><br><strong>P.S. This book kind of sparked the inspiration for one of his best book Under the Dome so go wild.</strong><br><br>Thanks for reading :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11087185617658809130" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16597289878392712125">Well, I just finished The Tommyknockers, and to be rather blunt...I'm disappointed. Really disappointed. <br/><br/>The beginning was good, entertaining, even hilarious at parts. When I got to Book II, it really started to drag for me. And when I mean drag....I just kept saying in my head as I read, "GET ON WITH IT ALREADY!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!" But also, I felt like it was a little all over the place. I consider myself to be one who at least has half a brain (Hell, I have a Master's degree, I would I hope I have</span>
  <span id="freeText16597289878392712125" style="display:none">Well, I just finished The Tommyknockers, and to be rather blunt...I'm disappointed. Really disappointed. <br /><br />The beginning was good, entertaining, even hilarious at parts. When I got to Book II, it really started to drag for me. And when I mean drag....I just kept saying in my head as I read, "GET ON WITH IT ALREADY!!!!!!!!!!!!!!!" But also, I felt like it was a little all over the place. I consider myself to be one who at least has half a brain (Hell, I have a Master's degree, I would I hope I have a brain), and consider myself to be someone who pays close attention when reading...but at times, I felt lost and my mind wandered from the story. I put the book down a few times and walked away just thinking I was reading it in the wrong environment or at the wrong time. However, I felt like this the whole time once I got into Book II. Book III picks up, and then I don't know if it is from the slagging of Book II, but it just felt like it kept going on, and on, and on, and on, and on, and on...Have you had enough now? I know I have. <br /><br />I guess I expected more to the story. Maybe I didn't enjoy it because I didn't feel anything for the characters, or the story, or just...any of it. I think this story could have been simplified and it would have been a more enjoyable read for me. And maybe, this one just wasn't for me. I LOVE alien crap....I'm an X-Files junkie...but The Tommyknockers just wasn't my cup of tea, I guess. </span>

<div id="review_2323232661" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2323232661" />
    <a title="The Face of Your Father" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/79119566-the-face-of-your-father"><img alt="The Face of Your Father" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597595315p2/79119566.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2323232661?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="The Face of Your Father" class="user" itemprop="url" name="The Face of Your Father" href="/user/show/79119566-the-face-of-your-father">The Face of Your Father</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2323232661" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1483439254069348198">There is a certain novel within Stephen King's bibliography that is treated like an adopted son that you hide underneath the stairs. What can be said about a novel that is hated by the majority and disowned by the author who wrote it? <br/><br/>Well.. I believe that 1987's small town tale 'The Tommyknockers' is as good, if not better, than the novel that came before it, 1986's thesis on the horror genre, 'IT'. <br/><br/>Let us dissect: 'The Tommyknockers' features everything you love about the traits of King. Are y</span>
  <span id="freeText1483439254069348198" style="display:none">There is a certain novel within Stephen King's bibliography that is treated like an adopted son that you hide underneath the stairs. What can be said about a novel that is hated by the majority and disowned by the author who wrote it? <br /><br />Well.. I believe that 1987's small town tale 'The Tommyknockers' is as good, if not better, than the novel that came before it, 1986's thesis on the horror genre, 'IT'. <br /><br />Let us dissect: 'The Tommyknockers' features everything you love about the traits of King. Are you a fan of small towns with a rich and detailed history, such as Derry and Castle Rock? Well, here is Haven. A town corrupted by the extraterrestrial presence within its borders, King doesn't give us the history of the town in as much detail as he does with Derry in 'IT', yet the snippets he does offer is fascinating. If you enjoyed the Derry Interludes from 'IT', check out the lesser known but equally as good "Tales of Haven"<br /><br />How about great child and female characters? Certainly you enjoyed The Losers Club a mere one year earlier, fan of Dolores Claiborne of Jessie Burlingame too, I bet. Well, Tommyknockers offers readers Hilly Brown and Ruth McCaulsand. Hilly Brown is a desperately needed character of 'The Tommyknockers'. Before his appearance, the novel possesses some of the bleakest, most dour, adult filled, depressing passages of King's career. Hilly Brown then steps on to the page to bring the humorous and passionate childhood desire of being a magician. King injects some much need levity into the story; like a nurse with a syringe, King expertly puts just the right amount of humor to make the reader take a needed breath. Ruth McCausland is a really underrated King female character. Her strength echoes throughout the pages, she is arguably the strongest character throughout the entire novel. A fighter and a leader with the courage to face off with any other of King's characters. <br /><br />Do you enjoy the love between Eddie Dean and Susannah from the Dark Tower series? Well, here is Bobbi Anderson and Jim Gardener. A love that spans across state lines, a love that reignites the will to live. <br /><br />King is known to address the struggles of addiction throughout his works. Yet, I'm not sure if it is done better, and more uncomfortably, than within 'The Tommyknockers'. There is a sense of something personal within King's words, much of what Jim Gardener goes through mirrors what the author was going through at the same time. It is raw, unpleasant, and bleak. The power behind the passages can not be denied.<br /><br />You want tension? King is an expert at tightening those screws, leaving the reader to scream for some relief. Just wait until you reach the chapter within 'The Tommyknockers' entitled "The Shed". King does not let up. <br /><br />Think of 'The Tommyknockers' as the sister novel to 'IT', much like his other works 'Desperation' and 'The Regulators'. The two novels share a lot of similar qualities and Tommyknockers benefits greatly if read directly after completing 'IT'. While 'IT' is the story of leaving childhood behind for good, 'The Tommyknockers' is about the bleak concept of surviving adulthood </span>
  <a data-text-id="1483439254069348198" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1702227329442499242">I'm an unabashed fan of this book. Through and through. In fact, I'd say it's one of the best books of his that I've ever read. Yes, I'm dead serious.<br/><br/>The pace is deliciously slow, building tension in waves and layers, rather than spurts and fits. The cosmic terror is enjoyably vague, yet the nature and logic of its machinations contains some truly eerie implications. And the characters? Don't even get me started...but, since I have already, they're amazing. Not one owner of a name is anything le</span>
  <span id="freeText1702227329442499242" style="display:none">I'm an unabashed fan of this book. Through and through. In fact, I'd say it's one of the best books of his that I've ever read. Yes, I'm dead serious.<br /><br />The pace is deliciously slow, building tension in waves and layers, rather than spurts and fits. The cosmic terror is enjoyably vague, yet the nature and logic of its machinations contains some truly eerie implications. And the characters? Don't even get me started...but, since I have already, they're amazing. Not one owner of a name is anything less than a real flesh-and-blood person. The red-haired girl who gives a ride to a certain main character hitching a long-distance was especially touching, and one I truly hope King returns to in another story someday.<br /><br />Before, during, and after reading this book, I was well-aware of this book's rocky reputation, and yet I read it without caring...and I was simply allured by it, right up through its final, haunting images. The way I see it - if you just didn't see the genuine charm of this book...well, it's too bad you can't share the breathless conversations about it with me. :)</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_78615336" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78615336" />
    <a title="Maciek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek"><img alt="Maciek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280839159p2/2969647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/78615336?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maciek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maciek" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek">Maciek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780450488351" href="/book/show/848436.The_Tommyknockers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=owned-books">owned-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=big-tomes">big-tomes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=own-in-hardcover">own-in-hardcover</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=own-in-paperback">own-in-paperback</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78615336" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8468970301704251524"><i>Late last night and the night before,<br/>Tommyknockers, Tommyknockers, knocking at the door.<br/>I want to go out, don't know if I can,<br/>'Cause I'm so afraid of the Tommyknocker man.</i><br/><br/>When Bobbi Anderson goes for a walk in the woods and trips over a small piece of metal, she doesn't know that she has only seconds left to live. Her life will not end, though; it will <i>change</i> in a way that she, a writer of popular westerns, is not even able to imagine. Determined to see what is in the earth, Bobbi starts digging</span>
  <span id="freeText8468970301704251524" style="display:none"><i>Late last night and the night before,<br />Tommyknockers, Tommyknockers, knocking at the door.<br />I want to go out, don't know if I can,<br />'Cause I'm so afraid of the Tommyknocker man.</i><br /><br />When Bobbi Anderson goes for a walk in the woods and trips over a small piece of metal, she doesn't know that she has only seconds left to live. Her life will not end, though; it will <i>change</i> in a way that she, a writer of popular westerns, is not even able to imagine. Determined to see what is in the earth, Bobbi starts digging and unknowingly bestows a curse upon the small town of Haven, maine. Because it's not an old bike or some boat that's sticking out of the ground; it's a spacecraft that has been there for millions of years. Bobbi's old friend, Jim Gardener, senses that she might be in danger and ventures to see her.<br /><br />Now, this sounds great, and truly would be, if the novel didn't go all around the place. Knocking at just over 700 pages, <i>The Tommyknockers</i> often meanders and looses focus, jumping and switching the narration like a drunk switches bars on Saturday night, or any night for that matter. If approximately 50 or a 100 pages were rewritten or edited, the novel would improve dramatically in pacing and plot structure.<br />There's some truly great stuff in <i>The Tommyknockers</i>; Gardener's scene at a poetry recitation combined with a cocktail party is truly great and displays the sheer raw power of King's writing. He wasn't writing with ink, he was writing with <i>rage</i> and it shows. Even though it was written in the 80's it looses none of its power. The usual small-town atmosphere is done well, and there's a lot of allusions to King's other works, such as <i>IT</i> or <i>Firestarter</i>. The change that the town of Haven undergoes, the dramatic conclusion and the beautiful epilogue are the shining points of this novel and deserve praise.<br /><br />It's a surprisingly poignant novel about aging, addiction and change that comes to us all with time. The lack of power to fight these forces is not the reason to stop fighting, even if the end result is almost certainly futile. And the Tommyknockers are real. Like King says in the preface - just watch the evening news. The new boss is the same as the old boss.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8468970301704251524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText2947309719100298747" style="display:none">One of these days, I'm going to go through all my old King books and write reviews, considering a massive chunk of them I had read long before Goodreads. BUT TODAY IS NOT THAT DAY!!<br /><br />For now, I'll add a mini review here and there as I write about them over on Bookstagram.<br />__________________________________________<br /><br />The Tommyknockers is one of the most divisive King novels, splitting Constant Readers into two groups - those that liked it and those that hated it. Hell, Uncle Stevie himself said it's an awful book.<br /><br />I don't agree.<br /><br />The Tommyknockers was during the worst of his addictions, written in a haze of cocaine and booze. It's essentially a bloated Lovecraftian Invasion of the Body Snatchers as written by the King. But.. there's just something about it. It's messy and raw and bleak and weird as fuck. It's also a harrowing, vivid look at addiction. The Tommyknockers is all over the damn place, yet I can't help but think of it as an important book in so many ways.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2084025291" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10518753305869839249">Awe shucks, such a missed opportunity by King here.<br/><br/>The Tommyknockers was a solid concept, but poorly executed. King himself has criticised this book as being one of, if not <i>the</i>, worst of his novels. He said it was the last book he wrote before cleaning up his act. In other words, it was written during a coke-fuelled binge of sorts. And it shows.<br/><br/>For me, this novel is really a book of thirds. <br/><br/>1/3. This section of the book was honestly some of the most engaging King writing I've ever read. Prior to</span>
  <span id="freeText10518753305869839249" style="display:none">Awe shucks, such a missed opportunity by King here.<br /><br />The Tommyknockers was a solid concept, but poorly executed. King himself has criticised this book as being one of, if not <i>the</i>, worst of his novels. He said it was the last book he wrote before cleaning up his act. In other words, it was written during a coke-fuelled binge of sorts. And it shows.<br /><br />For me, this novel is really a book of thirds. <br /><br />1/3. This section of the book was honestly some of the most engaging King writing I've ever read. Prior to starting Tommyknockers I'd heard much about how this book was one of King's least liked books by fans. But when I started reading the book, I thought everyone must have been off their Tommyknocker-rocker! The first third is a brilliant examination into the two main characters' minds, their lives. The characters are interesting, likeable and hateable alike. The writing is King at his best, and it's an incredible, tense, detailed introduction into what was a very juicy concept.<br /><br />2/3. Here the book collapses in on itself like a dying star. Suddenly King veers away from the two very interesting main characters, and introduces a whole shwack of characters out of nowhere. For the most part, none of them had any business being in the story. They didn't add anything to the essential grain of the idea. Rather, they cluttered up the story, screwed up the pace, and mostly, confused the hell out of me. I like to think this was where King was snorting coke off to the side of his laptop with a rolled up dollar bill in one hand while typing with his other hand. <br /><br />3/3. Here the book tries to return to it's first-third roots, but by now it's far too late. You've lost the reader in all the confusion that was the coke-infused middle section of the book. King attempts to bring together everything here, but since he introduced so many different characters and random sub plots in the middle part of the book, it was like he was really grasping at straws trying to figure out how the hell to incorporate it all together.<br /><br />Final analysis. There's a fantastic book in here somewhere. This 550ish page novel should be cut back to about 300, losing much of the middle junk, and you'd have a helluva story here. <br /><br />The Tommyknockers felt a bit like a cross between Stranger Things and Under the Dome. That said, Under the Dome was far superior in execution to the concept of having a town trapped under the influence of an unknown force. I feel like Under the Dome was in some ways a chance at redemption for this concept for King. <br /><br />I often hear of authors who wish they could go back with a red marker and edit some of their novels. I would imagine King would want to go back and do this with Tommyknockers. If you exclude the random middle third of the novel, there is a delicious mixture of suspense, horror, supernatural, realism, and all the other stuff that makes King a staple on most bookshelves. <br /><br />My two star rating appears harsh. It's more like 2.5 stars. If King went back with a re-do he'd likely be able to boost this into one of his greater novels. Alas, time moves on, and so has King. There are still many many many King's left for me to read, so I'm not going to dwell any longer on this. <br /><br />Onward.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10518753305869839249" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kMUbEn9xlJUcTXftE2VkUqR3L6Dy6CCl52CU4gGXVIQzQ9oEmJLBdyOngxv67JBry6Q/uMYaVPzjQlvH7QNV+Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gJMLQ3t4ugnyR02Jju0s0cCM1kTANBODii4OVoVPvhwjFcpVnJvv682tuX9nZNjor1/GXPTGZ9qODMFzadu/YQ==')})
  }
//]]>

Louis recounts how he became a vampire at the hands of the radiant and sinister Lestat and how he became indoctrinated, unwillingly, into the vampire way of life. His story ebbs and flows through the streets of New Orleans, defining crucial moments such as his discovery of the exquisite lost young child Claudia, wanting not to hurt but to comfort her with the last breaths of humanity he has inside. Yet, he makes Claudia a vampire, trapping her womanly passion, will, and intelligence inside the body of a small child. Louis and Claudia form a seemingly unbreakable alliance and even &quot;settle down&quot; for a while in the opulent French Quarter. Louis remembers Claudia\'s struggle to understand herself and the hatred they both have for Lestat that sends them halfway across the world to seek others of their kind. Louis and Claudia are desperate to find somewhere they belong, to find others who understand, and someone who knows what and why they are.\n\nLouis and Claudia travel Europe, eventually coming to Paris and the ragingly successful Theatre des Vampires--a theatre of vampires pretending to be mortals pretending to be vampires. Here they meet the magnetic and ethereal Armand, who brings them into a whole society of vampires. But Louis and Claudia find that finding others like themselves provides no easy answers and in fact presents dangers they scarcely imagined.\n\nOriginally begun as a short story, the book took off as Anne wrote it, spinning the tragic and triumphant life experiences of a soul. As well as the struggles of its characters, Interview captures the political and social changes of two continents. The novel also introduces Lestat, Anne\'s most enduring character, a heady mixture of attraction and revulsion. The book, full of lush description, centers on the themes of immortality, change, loss, sexuality, and power.\nsource: annerice.com<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13920026595431631689\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3389.Stephen_King">About Stephen King</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3389.Stephen_King"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1362814142p3/3389.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/3389.Stephen_King">Stephen King
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11311438374921250255">Stephen Edwin King was born the second son of Donald and Nellie Ruth Pillsbury King. After his father left them when Stephen was two, he and his older brother, David, were raised by his mother. Parts of his childhood were spent in Fort Wayne, Indiana, where his father's family was at the time, and in Stratford, Connecticut. When Stephen was eleven, his mother brought her children back to Durham, M</span>
  <span id="freeText11311438374921250255" style="display:none">Stephen Edwin King was born the second son of Donald and Nellie Ruth Pillsbury King. After his father left them when Stephen was two, he and his older brother, David, were raised by his mother. Parts of his childhood were spent in Fort Wayne, Indiana, where his father's family was at the time, and in Stratford, Connecticut. When Stephen was eleven, his mother brought her children back to Durham, Maine, for good. Her parents, Guy and Nellie Pillsbury, had become incapacitated with old age, and Ruth King was persuaded by her sisters to take over the physical care of them. Other family members provided a small house in Durham and financial support. After Stephen's grandparents passed away, Mrs. King found work in the kitchens of Pineland, a nearby residential facility for the mentally challenged.<br /><br />Stephen attended the grammar school in Durham and Lisbon Falls High School, graduating in 1966. From his sophomore year at the University of Maine at Orono, he wrote a weekly column for the school newspaper, THE MAINE CAMPUS. He was also active in student politics, serving as a member of the Student Senate. He came to support the anti-war movement on the Orono campus, arriving at his stance from a conservative view that the war in Vietnam was unconstitutional. He graduated in 1970, with a B.A. in English and qualified to teach on the high school level. A draft board examination immediately post-graduation found him 4-F on grounds of high blood pressure, limited vision, flat feet, and punctured eardrums.<br /><br />He met Tabitha Spruce in the stacks of the Fogler Library at the University, where they both worked as students; they married in January of 1971. As Stephen was unable to find placement as a teacher immediately, the Kings lived on his earnings as a laborer at an industrial laundry, and her student loan and savings, with an occasional boost from a short story sale to men's magazines.<br /><br />Stephen made his first professional short story sale ("The Glass Floor") to <i>Startling Mystery Stories</i> in 1967. Throughout the early years of his marriage, he continued to sell stories to men's magazines. Many were gathered into the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10628.Night_Shift" title="Night Shift" rel="nofollow noopener">Night Shift</a> collection or appeared in other anthologies.<br /><br />In the fall of 1971, Stephen began teaching English at Hampden Academy, the public high school in Hampden, Maine. Writing in the evenings and on the weekends, he continued to produce short stories and to work on novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11311438374921250255" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
